          <div class="Pos-r Z-0 article-container legacy" data-template="desktop" id="article-hitler-got-mustache-200100278" style="background-color:white;min-height:315px;">
           <div class="Pos-r sticky-container print-article" style="min-height:315px;">
            <div class="article-inner-container" style="max-width:1280px;">
             <div class="inset-container Mx-a">
              <div class="GridTable">
               <div class="D-tbr row-template">
                <div class="GridTable-Cell Va-t col-left">
                 <div class="content" style="width:825px;min-width:825px;">
                  <noscript>
                  </noscript>
                  <noscript>
                  </noscript>
                  <div class="GridTable grid-content">
                   <div class="D-tbr row-content">
                    <div class="GridTable-Cell Va-t col-left Pend-20" style="width:305px;max-width:305px;">
                     <h1 class="Font-A-6-110 title W-100" id="article-aft-title-hitler-got-mustache-200100278">
                      How Hitler Got That Mustache, and What Else We Learned From World Wars
                     </h1>
                     <div class="attribution-component D-b W-100" id="article-aft-attribution-hitler-got-mustache-200100278" style="max-width:305px;">
                      <div class="author-info author-info-text-first Pt-10 W-100 Bd-t Bd-b Bdstart-0 Bdend-0">
                       <div class="GridTable W-100">
                        <div class="D-tbr">
                         <div class="GridTable-Cell Va-t provider-info">
                          <a class="provider-logo" href="http://www.thewrap.com" target="_blank">
                           <img alt="The Wrap" class="Maw-100" src="https://s.yimg.com/lo/api/res/1.2/ippKmAWyPPgFeYOkbQie_g--/YXBwaWQ9bWFnYXppbmVzO2ZpPWZpdDt3PTE1MA--/https://s.yimg.com/os/publish-images/movies/2013-09-09/f3881ede-78dc-41ad-b9f0-c1efba3f8c67_Logo_Small.jpg"/>
                          </a>
                          <div class="provider-alias C-n">
                           <a class="Font-C-2-140 Fw-b" href="http://www.thewrap.com" target="_blank">
                            The Wrap
                           </a>
                          </div>
                         </div>
                        </div>
                       </div>
                       <div class="GridTable W-100">
                        <div class="D-tbr">
                         <div class="GridTable-Cell Va-t">
                          <cite class="byline col D-b Fs-n" style="padding-bottom:15px;">
                           <span class="Font-C-2-140 Fw-n D-b">
                            Tim Molloy
                           </span>
                           <time class="date Font-C-2-140 Fw-n D-b">
                            <span>
                             August 5, 2014
                            </span>
                           </time>
                          </cite>
                         </div>
                        </div>
                       </div>
                      </div>
                     </div>
                    </div>
                    <div class="GridTable-Cell Va-t col-right" style="max-width:500px;">
                     <div class="text-body-wrapper W-100 Font-B-1-160">
                      <noscript>
                      </noscript>
                      <div class="text-body W-100 Font-B-1-160" id="article-aft-text-body-hitler-got-mustache-200100278" style="color:#222222;max-width:500px;">
                       <div class="body read-more-cut">
                        <div class="pre-cut" style="height:auto;margin-bottom:1.5em;">
                         <p class="Mah-100 Maw-100 Mb-20">
                          <img alt="How Hitler Got That Mustache, and What Else We Learned From World Wars" class="Mah-100 Maw-100 W-100" src="https://s.yimg.com/lo/api/res/1.2/Bi6ifBa0KO8y19NpGw4yMg--/YXBwaWQ9bWFnYXppbmVzO3c9NTAwO2ZpPWZpdA--/https://s.yimg.com/os/en_US/News/TheWrap/How_Hitler_Got_That_Mustache_-cd9c940c8774a1a9fca04efba16292f9"/>
                          <span class="Font-C-1-160 D-ib Mt-4" style="color:#777;">
                           How Hitler Got That Mustache, and What Else We Learned From World Wars
                          </span>
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          History's The World Wars begins with German soldiers in a trench, pulling gas masks over their faces. A skinny private with a bushy mustache finds that his mask won't seal because his whiskers are too thick. As soon as the gas clears, he takes out a knife to cut his mustache into a compact rectangle.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          The little-known account of how Adolf Hitler got his strange mustache is only one of the surprising stories told in the series, which is Emmy-nominated for Outstanding Writing for Nonfiction, Outstanding Sound Editing for Nonfiction Programming, and Outstanding Documentary or Nonfiction Series.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          <strong>
                           Also read:
                          </strong>
                          <a class="also-read-counter" data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:07f00ad7-6b3e-3d41-b014-1988f7a278e2;t9:18;itc:0" href="http://www.thewrap.com/auschwitz-selfie-outrage-but-youre-supposed-to-take-pictures/" target="_blank">
                           Auschwitz Selfie Outrage: But You're Supposed to Take Pictures
                          </a>
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          Executive producer Stephen David, whose Men Who Built America was also Emmy-nominated last year, talked to us about how he brought two World Wars to life. The three-part series blends re-enactments, archival footage and interviews with some of the most famous warriors of the modern era.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          <b>
                           Why begin the series as you did?
                           <br/>
                          </b>
                          We were doing a lot of research and when we found that story, we thought, oh right. The mustache. It's the iconic thing about Hitler. We could just open the show with it. It was our favorite fact that we found out.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          <strong>
                           Also read:
                          </strong>
                          <a data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:07f00ad7-6b3e-3d41-b014-1988f7a278e2;t9:18;itc:0" href="http://www.thewrap.com/tv/article/emmys-how-men-who-built-america-compare-steve-jobs-113011/" target="_blank">
                           Emmys: How The Men Who Built America Compare to Steve Jobs
                          </a>
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          <b>
                           He kept the mustache long after World War I ended. Was that his way of saying he was always at war? Or did he always fear a gas attack?
                           <br/>
                          </b>
                          I think in actuality after World War I that was a fashion, and a lot of people did that. Between World War I and World War II, that was a fashion that a lot of people had. And being that Hitler had it, he kind of killed that fashion.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          <b>
                           How did you research this film? Is everything precisely accurate, or dramatized? I've also read, for example, that he was ordered to cut the mustache.
                           <br/>
                          </b>
                          We're trying to be as factual as we can. Sometimes we have to compress time. He wasn't ordered to shave the mustache, but actually he shaved the mustache while he was in the hospital. Budget-wise, it would have been tough to film there as well as in the trench.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          We don't have very big budgets to make these. It's always a big challenge to see how we're going to pull off two wars with the budgets we have. It's more in line with a reality show than a scripted show.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          <b>
                           How do you find people who look like the younger versions of these historic figures? Or even know they look like people from a hundred years ago, like Woodrow Wilson?
                           <br/>
                          </b>
                          Sometimes we get lucky and we get people who already know they look like these people. Woodrow Wilson, that guy apparently was at an event for Woodrow Wilson and somebody came up and said, Oh my God, you look just like Woodrow Wilson! The hardest part to find was Hitler. We did a lot of casting, I remember our casting notice was picked up online at one point and a lot of sites were saying, It's finally good to look like Hitler!
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          We could not find a Hitler. Anybody that came in, they all felt very cartoonish. There was a guy from The Men Who Built America who had a small part, but he was a really good actor. I remember looking at his picture and thinking, I really think that guy could be Hitler. He embodied him so well, and then we brought in makeup and hair people and we probably worked for a week on all of it and there's a lot of prosthetics. We did a lot of work on him.
                         </p>
                        </div>
                        <div class="post-cut W-100" id="post-cut" style="transition-property:none;-webkit-transition-property:none;transition-duration:10s;-webkit-transition-duration:10s;transition-timing-function:ease-in;-webkit-transition-timing-function:ease-in;overflow:hidden;max-height:0;max-width:500px;">
                         <p>
                          <strong>
                           You talked to people like John McCain, Colin Powell, Stanley McChrystal and Dick Cheney. It's interesting to see people who are sometimes controversial figures today giving perspective on these figures from generations ago. For example, we hear Sen. McCain talk about Patton being egotistical.
                           <br/>
                          </strong>
                          We never want to skew Republican or Democrat or this is right or this is wrong.  The biggest thing that we decide when we're putting something in is whether they speak about something passionately. You can kind of tell. And those are the bites that, when you're watching, feel the best. I mean McChrystal just knew so much. That's why he's in it so much.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          There was a bite where Cheney said, between the wars, about isolationism: What, were we just supposed to go to war when there was no war even started? It was like, Wait, wait, wait. Did he just say that? We put that in.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          The post
                          <a data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:07f00ad7-6b3e-3d41-b014-1988f7a278e2;t9:18;itc:0" href="http://www.thewrap.com/how-hitler-got-that-mustache/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank">
                           How Hitler Got That Mustache, and What Else We Learned From World Wars
                          </a>
                          appeared first on
                          <a data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:07f00ad7-6b3e-3d41-b014-1988f7a278e2;t9:18;itc:0" href="http://www.thewrap.com" rel="nofollow" target="_blank">
                           TheWrap
                          </a>
                          .
                         </p>

Leary Become A Rock Star?\" width=\"540\" height=\"360\"\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EI&#x2019;m pretty sure Denis Leary did not intend his new show \u003Ci\u003ESex&amp;Drugs&amp;Rock&amp;Roll\u003C\u002Fi\u003E to be interpreted this way, but, hey, rock &amp; roll makes up its rules as it goes along: This FX series premiering Thursday makes a very good case for the notion that there&#x2019;s no such thing as authenticity in rock music.&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ENever one to go for subtlety, Leary stars as a musician named Johnny Rock, a 50 year-old cult star who, we&#x2019;re supposed to believe, could have been a big star if only he hadn&#x2019;t feuded with the Keith Richards to his Mick Jagger, his co-writer\u002Flead-guitarist Flash (John Corbett, whose achievement here is extending his wastrel-musician character from\u003Ci\u003E Parenthood\u003C\u002Fi\u003E into complete degeneracy). Johnny, who still struts around with a rooster hairdo lifted from the head of Rod Stewart circa \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fwatch?v=HLBFyS2hlhw\"\u003E\u003Ci\u003EGasoline Alley\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, broke up the band and has been grasping at fame for about three decades now, when his new meal ticket suddenly arrives.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThat meal ticket takes the form of his long-lost daughter, who&#x2019;s got the youth, pipes, and Instagrammed body to give anyone&#x2019;s career a boost, and if you think that sounds crude, that is exactly what show-creator Leary wants you to think when you look at and listen to Gigi. She&#x2019;s played by Elizabeth Gillies, a Broadway and Nickelodeon vet who&#x2019;s immediately convincing as a young professional with daddy issues who is not above using Johnny as cynically as he plans to use her. A warm family sitcom \u003Ci\u003ESex&amp;Drugs\u003C\u002Fi\u003E is not.&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cfigure class=\"tmblr-embed tmblr-full\"\u003E\n \u003Ciframe width=\"540\" height=\"304\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FAsmxcw4iFME?feature=oembed\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ENeither is it a very funny sitcom. In \u003Ci\u003ERescue Me,\u003C\u002Fi\u003E Leary had the benefit of co-creator Peter Tolan and a deep bench of talented co-stars to whom he was willing to cede the spotlight. This time around, Leary surrounds himself with good actors who are very much the actor-equivalent of a back-up band, and he crowds Gillies for the spotlight in every scene they share. This is not wise when the punchlines he gives himself include, &#x201C;I wanna blow Bruce Jenner&#x201D; and &#x201C;I&#x2019;m not writing some auto-tune Katy Perry bulls&#x2013;t.&#x201D; Maybe I&#x2019;d feel more charitable about this had Leary not co-opted the title of a \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fwatch?v=2TBSqS4yMrM\"\u003Egreat Ian Dury song\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and failed to make his own, self-written theme song one-thousandth as good.&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EYou&#x2019;re supposed to think Johnny Rock is a jerk who&#x2019;s actually heroic because he&#x2019;s fighting for the rebel spirit of rock, but I&#x2019;ll bet there are a lot of viewers who&#x2019;ll identify more with Gigi, a realist who&#x2019;s fighting for the freedom that comes with being a pop star nowadays. In Gigi&#x2019;s cosmos &#x2014; and mine &#x2014; Katy Perry is as legit as Steven Tyler, or Jon Bon Jovi, or any other rocker the show invokes.&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cfigure class=\"tmblr-full\"\u003E\n \u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fg\u002Fimages\u002Fspaceball.gif\" alt=\"image\" width=\"540\" height=\"335\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002Ff7ccab73b890d979bdf6a5d89cb1ae18cb9fe6c8.jpg\"\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ETV and rock &amp; roll have a history as long as both mediums. Not just Elvis Presley wiggling from the waist-up on \u003Ci\u003EThe Ed Sullivan Show\u003C\u002Fi\u003E, but also Ricky Nelson smuggling future Presley guitarist James Burton onto the otherwise-anodyne \u003Ci\u003EThe Adventures of Ozzie and Harriet\u003C\u002Fi\u003E (1952-&#x2019;66). From \u003Ci\u003EThe Monkees\u003C\u002Fi\u003E to \u003Ci\u003EEmpire\u003C\u002Fi\u003E, television shows that use pop music as a metaphor for freedom, fame, and wealth have always been compromised projects, some better than others.&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ENow that rock is merely one genre among many from which a singer like Gigi plucks a melody or tempo, the bottom-line message of \u003Ci\u003ESex&amp;Drugs&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2014; that one kind of music is privileged with a unique truth &#x2014; is undercut by what&#x2019;s actually onscreen: the spectacle of a deluded rocker being aced by a shrewd pop-ster.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESex&amp;Drugs&amp;Rock&amp;Roll \u003Ci\u003Eairs Thursday at 10 p.m. on FX. Hitler Got That Mustache, and What Else We Learned From World Wars","summary":"History's The World Wars begins with German soldiers in a trench, pulling gas masks over their faces. The little-known account of how Adolf Hitler got his strange mustache is only one of the surprising stories told in the series, which is Emmy-nominated for Outstanding Writing for Nonfiction, Outstanding Sound Editing for Nonfiction Programming, and Outstanding Documentary or Nonfiction Series. Executive producer Stephen David, whose Men Who Built America was also Emmy-nominated last year, talked to us about how he brought two World Wars to life. It's the iconic thing about Hitler.","type":"story","body":"\u003Cp\u003EHistory&apos;s &#x201C;The World Wars&#x201D; begins with German soldiers in a trench, pulling gas masks over their faces. A skinny private with a bushy mustache finds that his mask won&apos;t seal because his whiskers are too thick. As soon as the gas clears, he takes out a knife to cut his mustache into a compact rectangle.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EThe little-known account of how Adolf Hitler got his strange mustache is only one of the surprising stories told in the series, which is Emmy-nominated for Outstanding Writing for Nonfiction, Outstanding Sound Editing for Nonfiction Programming, and Outstanding Documentary or Nonfiction Series.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EAlso read:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E \u003Ca class=\"also-read-counter\" href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.thewrap.com\u002Fauschwitz-selfie-outrage-but-youre-supposed-to-take-pictures\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003E&#x2018;Auschwitz Selfie&#x2019; Outrage: But You&apos;re Supposed to Take Pictures\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EExecutive producer Stephen David, whose &#x201C;Men Who Built America&#x201D; was also Emmy-nominated last year, talked to us about how he brought two World Wars to life. The three-part series blends re-enactments, archival footage and interviews with some of the most famous warriors of the modern era.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EWhy begin the series as you did?\u003Cbr\u003E \u003C\u002Fb\u003EWe were doing a lot of research and when we found that story, we thought, oh right. The mustache. It&apos;s the iconic thing about Hitler. We could just open the show with it. It was our favorite fact that we found out.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EAlso read:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.thewrap.com\u002Ftv\u002Farticle\u002Femmys-how-men-who-built-america-compare-steve-jobs-113011\u002F\" target=\"_blank\"\u003EEmmys: How &#x2018;The Men Who Built America&#x2019; Compare to Steve Jobs\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EHe kept the mustache long after World War I ended. Was that his way of saying he was always at war? Or did he always fear a gas attack?\u003Cbr\u003E \u003C\u002Fb\u003EI think in actuality after World War I that was a fashion, and a lot of people did that. Between World War I and World War II, that was a fashion that a lot of people had. And being that Hitler had it, he kind of killed that fashion.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EHow did you research this film? Is everything precisely accurate, or dramatized? I&apos;ve also read, for example, that he was ordered to cut the mustache.\u003Cbr\u003E \u003C\u002Fb\u003EWe&apos;re trying to be as factual as we can. Sometimes we have to compress time. He wasn&apos;t ordered to shave the mustache, but actually he shaved the mustache while he was in the hospital. Budget-wise, it would have been tough to film there as well as in the trench.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWe don&apos;t have very big budgets to make these. It&apos;s always a big challenge to see how we&apos;re going to pull off two wars with the budgets we have. It&apos;s more in line with a reality show than a scripted show.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EHow do you find people who look like the younger versions of these historic figures? Or even know they look like people from a hundred years ago, like Woodrow Wilson?\u003Cbr\u003E \u003C\u002Fb\u003ESometimes we get lucky and we get people who already know they look like these people. Woodrow Wilson, that guy apparently was at an event for Woodrow Wilson and somebody came up and said, &#x2018;Oh my God, you look just like Woodrow Wilson!&#x2019; The hardest part to find was Hitler. We did a lot of casting, I remember our casting notice was picked up online at one point and a lot of sites were saying, &#x201C;It&apos;s finally good to look like Hitler!&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWe could not find a Hitler. Anybody that came in, they all felt very cartoonish. There was a guy from &#x201C;The Men Who Built America&#x201D; who had a small part, but he was a really good actor. I remember looking at his picture and thinking, &#x2018;I really think that guy could be Hitler.&#x2019; He embodied him so well, and then we brought in makeup and hair people and we probably worked for a week on all of it and there&apos;s a lot of prosthetics. We did a lot of work on him.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EYou talked to people like John McCain, Colin Powell, Stanley McChrystal and Dick Cheney. It&apos;s interesting to see people who are sometimes controversial figures today giving perspective on these figures from generations ago. For example, we hear Sen. McCain talk about Patton being egotistical.\u003Cbr\u003E \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003EWe never want to skew Republican or Democrat or this is right or this is wrong. &#x2026; The biggest thing that we decide when we&apos;re putting something in is whether they speak about something passionately. You can kind of tell. And those are the bites that, when you&apos;re watching, feel the best. I mean McChrystal just knew so much. That&apos;s why he&apos;s in it so much.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EThere was a bite where Cheney said, between the wars, about isolationism: What, were we just supposed to go to war when there was no war even started? It was like, &#x2018;Wait, wait, wait. Did he just say that?&#x2019; We put that in.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EThe post \u003Ca rel=\"nofollow\"
